And how to get rid of them, that you sit the patient in front of you and tie his neck with a turban then turn his lips and look at the two veins which you see one on the right side of the lip and the other on the left side and determine from them by their blueness.
And also because of their being two blue and narrow veins that you cut them with a severed cut, if they are difficult for you to determine what they are then go ahead and cut the larger one of them and show it to him, and do the same with the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two sputa under the tongue, their benefit is to remove them after bleeding the throat to evacuate the mucus that forms in the pharynx from diseases of the laryngitis and mouth diseases. The manner of removing them is to sit the operator facing the sun with his patient and raise his tongue and look under it from one side a sputa and from the other side a sputa and their color turns black.
And remove them and preserve [the patient] do not be concerned with cutting them, because underneath them are veins, so perhaps bleeding will flow from those veins. As for the three veins that bleed in the armpit, they are what has been customary to bleed in people altogether, and their bleeding is on two sides.
Either insert a thin or olive-tipped needle into the vein, or slit it with a fine-bladed lancet and this is its shape.
It is wide as you see, suitable for opening the protruding and swollen veins that are visible and contain thick and dark blood. This is the shape of the olive-tipped lancet:.
And this lancet is narrower and thinner, suitable for bleeding the varicose veins that contain thin and yellowish blood. This is the shape of the fine-bladed lancet:.
This fine-bladed lancet is suitable for slitting, and there are various types of it according to the size and narrowness of the veins. And this may be used as a model for others, and among artisans, it is well-known.
And as for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit in bleeding is that it attracts blood from the abscesses that form under the throat and neck below the chest and belly. It should be necessary for the bleeder when he bleds to warn him and to be on the head of the vein because underneath it are arteries. So if he erred and increased in inserting the lancet, cut that artery.
There occurs hemorrhage, therefore its obstruction should not be with a needle of stitches, but its obstruction should be with an incision by a lancet. If the bleeding tissue does not become clearly visible then you must take heed and change to another or seek part of it.
Or you should ligate the place with a tendon of the arm, for it is clear and you cut it with a lancet as we said. If you wanted its obstruction by itself then before taking hold of the arm you must explore the place until you know the place of the pulse then mark on it with ink.
Then you bind the arm and cut the vein a cutting incision with a lancet as we said. And you should search so that the blow may fall far away from the place of the artery. And if when you tighten your bandage you see inflation in the place where you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery, therefore beware it.
And if you see the blood at the incision bubbling like urine of an infant and it was thin red then know that it is from the artery's blood, therefore hasten to stop it. Place your finger on it for a long time then withdraw your finger. If the bleeding stops, tighten the arm.
And leave him, and warn the attendant from neglecting it, let it be on his neck or not move it for a number of days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and it is pressing you and has not come to your aid with medicine then cut the artery if it appears to you, because its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a piece of onion peel and cut it in half and press one half on the place of the vein with a tight grasp with a bandage and a tourniquet until another day. If the bleeding stops, otherwise treat as we mentioned earlier by placing stopping pads on the hemorrhage, and cutting its blood is not difficult most of the time.
For a small wound in the arm where suturing is possible, let him know. And for the blue vein: this is beneficial if he prevents it from drawing blood from the top of the head and the lower body to a place that is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic as we mentioned.
It is advisable for the surgeon to be aware of the nerves, because if they are cut under them then increased sewing in the wound will cause numbness in it which may remain unhealed. And this nerve is often felt by touch.
If it is small in some people and thin so that it cannot be seen, then make the incision for your surgery in a lengthwise direction with the needle, and avoid the nerve. If the vein lies between two nerves, cut the vein longitudinally. And as for the cephalic vein, this is beneficial if he prevents it from drawing blood from the head.
It is beneficial for the illnesses of the eyes and it is necessary to cut this vein particularly if you want to cut it; with two stitches of olive oil needle or with a broad rosemary needle because it is the most safe of all veins since there is no artery nor nerve under it.
Unless it is necessary for you to avoid with the needle only the head of the muscle and look for the soft place, and it will not harm him if he does not hit with the first blow that he go back on this with a cut several times unless maybe it becomes swollen in some people if he did not cut with the first blow.
But that swelling will not harm him anything. And as for the manner of incision and its complications, and what should be done to repair it, first you should know that an incision is either used only for maintaining health.
Or it is used in diseases, and that which is used for maintaining health and its continuity, and the prevention of occurrence of diseases, then let the incision be one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the basilic, or the median, and let the incision be at the beginning of spring if there are signs of swelling.
Punction shall be performed on Sunday or Tuesday after the day has gone three hours. And as for punction which is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time but when necessity and need demand it by night or day.
And in every hour and in every time, the young should not be punctured until they have come fourteen years, nor should the elderly who have exceeded sixty years be punctured. If someone is determined to puncture for any reason.
It should be cleaned with a green instrument before puncturing, if there is much stagnant blood in it so that it does not adhere to the vessels when punctured from the belly, superfluous badness which harms the principal organs. Nor should the drunkard or the intoxicated person be punctured until that has passed away from them.
And one should also warn of puncturing after the menstrual period and vomiting and diarrhea and excessive coitus and fatigue and exercise and vigilance and fasting, and all that exhausts strength from a bodily or mental matter. Then look into the thinning of the humors before this if the blood is thick.
By means of food and drink and medicine if possible, then he enters the bath, if it is not prevented by an obstacle or some forms of exercise in order that his blood become clear and to make the puncture in the morning chest as we said, and that he intends to clear his chest from all bad mental obstructions.
Such as worries and anger and fear, and all bodily obstructions like fatigue and excessive lifting and coitus and the like. And he prepares his surroundings with things that people are accustomed to use from kinds of perfume and pleasant smells and pleasures and the like, each person according to what is possible for him.
Then the puncurer sits on a higher cushion than that which the patient sits on, then he extends his arm and the puncurer smooths it with his hand two or three times then tightens the bandage with forceps and twists it two or three times while being moderate in the tightening.
Because tension, when excessive, prevents bleeding and also when relaxed prevents it; then after the tension it is advisable that the surgeon rubs with both hands together on each other.
Until the blood vessels swell and become palpable, then he cleanses the cut with a gentle application of old oil; then he places his left thumb on top of the blood vessel under the place he wants to stop it so that the blood doesn't escape from the cut. Because of the blood vessels that are like a tendon which clings to the needle when cut, and those which are filled with air so that when you place the instrument on them they collapse beneath it and block the bleeding, and if the bleeding is opened it becomes narrow.
So the surgeon should be firm and cautious in all of this, then he lowers the needle; if it opens the blood vessel at that spot, otherwise he repeats it once again a little below or above that place with some effort unless the spot swells.
If the wound becomes swollen or festers, leave it for a day or two and do not pull tight the bandage; perhaps it may bring a hot abscess and he will not be able to go to the bathhouse, then he repeats the incision if he wishes, if the puncture was small and the bleeding is fine.
And be afraid that the amount of blood you want will not come out, so reinsert the needle into the same hole with care for straightness and increase the opening a little; do this with effort before the spot becomes swollen, because in many people the area becomes swollen even at small openings.
And if you see that it has become swollen, do not touch it at all because it will not help you; put on him something of the old oil's resin and it makes the bleeding easier and is better in this place than the oil itself or any other ointment.
Apply oil of aloes in the process of letting out blood from veins if the bleeding is difficult. The antidote of Feroq and Shakhsinaya may be used, by placing one of them on the wound; then the blood will flow and become clear if it was thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of venesection, it often happens especially to those who have only done it that one time or whose vein was small; be quick and put a sponge soaked in warm water and salt on the swelling for an hour; then it will resolve.
It is advisable to do this after the blood has come out completely from the vein itself or another one, if something dark or green remains in the place for days then it will not harm that; but if you wish, put a little of dissolved alum and salt on it.
Or something of fumitory juice and the like, often a swelling and inflammation occur when letting out blood from the basilic vein; put your hand on it, if you feel it soften under your touch then that is a bad inflammation, beware not to put anything on it as we mentioned.
Perhaps blood of the arteries will flow from it, but compress it with what is in its place so that it thickens then treat it with other treatments until it heals; and you should let out from every person as much blood as his strength allows and as much as appears to be the dominant color of the blood.
And if the blood is black then let it come out until it becomes red, and similarly if you see it thick then let it flow until it thins, and similarly if it is hot then let its heat go away; and it is advisable for someone who is full and strong to need to let out the blood all at once.
It is advisable to make the venesection of the vein wide and for the one who cuts it to be wide; and whoever is weak then do the opposite of that, and let it out in installments and have a small puncture; and what is best used in cutting the vein is to make it inclined, curved, and sliced not pierced.
This type of incision is safe from bleeding and nerve cut, and it is Ahmad and more perfect than the superficial and deep incision. If he takes it when making a wound, it should be fed with something soaked in rose water or almonds before making the wound.
If he is melancholic and blood comes out in three or four times, if his temperament is relaxed, then take before the incision a bread soaked in low wine or honey flavored with saffron or rose water.
If the superficial wound occurs during incision and its cause is the great exit of blood, then he should be given water to drink and thin rosewater drink, and use saffron as an ointment for his chest, and use all what was mentioned in the division about the superficial wound in the chapter on boils.
What happens because of a vomiting, and as for who wants to calm his arm and drain his blood again, then it should be drained little by little according to the strength in consecutive days.
And as for who wants to calm his arm and drain his blood again, and if his body is strong then he should do this after seven or eight hours of the first incision. And as for who wants to attract blood from his body to the opposite side that it inclined towards, then he should be taken care of on the second or third day.
And as for who has a lot of blood in his body that has boiled and heated up and made fever, then the blood should be taken out from him all at once and the great amount should be drained and the incision should be enlarged so that the boil is exposed after it has been matured to all the conditions of the incision. And you should put your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing, so that death does not occur at the place of the boil, for this often happens if the incisor is ignorant and forgetfulness occurs. And it should not be done if you wanted to calm the arm and drain the blood again.
If the mouth of the artery becomes closed and it is difficult for the blood to come out, one should press upon it with great force or twist it strongly. This indeed makes it very favorable. Either leave it until it clears again, or else draw away from the cut with a lancet what has congealed in the mouth of the artery.
Or take some salt and hold it to him, dissolved in water, or take some faruq or shakhzinaya antidote and smear it on him lightly until the blood comes out. If the artery has become inflamed then leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the inflammation subsides. If necessity demands that you let blood again, then either bleed above that place or else bleed in another arm or artery.
And as for bleeding the vein of the arm, it is done instead of the akeheel and basliq if they are not found or are hidden, since it is compounded from them. The manner of bleeding it that he puts his hand into hot water until the cloth becomes red and the artery shows itself distinctly then ties above it with a band tightly but not too tight.
Then bleed the artery a little obliquely, neither too high nor too low, and let the incision be wide. Let your cut be above the wrist joint a little. If it is difficult for the blood to come out then put the hand again into the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow in the water.
Until your need is satisfied. And if you are in the days of summer, perhaps you will not need to put the hand again into hot water, but rather let the blood flow in the warm water more often in winter time. Bleeding this artery is safer than all other arteries. Because there is no vein that runs beneath it nor an artery. And as for bleeding the basilic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver afflictions. The manner of doing it is to tie the upper part of the arm with a band or with your own hand after putting it into hot water until the vein swells up.
Because there is no vein that runs beneath it nor an artery. And as for bleeding the basilic vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for liver afflictions. The manner of doing it is to tie the upper part of the arm with a band or with your own hand after putting it into hot water until the vein swells up. The surgeon should make sure he feels the area very well and then distinguish it somewhat by cutting it a little bit, but if he cuts it completely, that will not harm him. He must be careful not to press his knife too hard, as there is a nerve under the skin of the fingers in this location, which is clearly visible from the flesh. Then return your hand to the hot water.
And let the blood run in it; for if you do not put them in the hot water, the blood will clot in the vein and refuse to flow. If you drew out as much blood as is needed, place on the vein oil and salt so that it does not become stuck quickly.
And you should also do the same with every narrow branch. As for usefulness, separate it from the left hand; for it is useful in diseases of the spleen, and similarly do what was done with the second one. And as for bleeding the veins of the vein of the sciatic nerve, its benefit is for diseases that are in the lower body, such as uterine issues.
And hold back menstruation and kidney diseases and chronic wounds of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments. And how to bleed it; that his leg enters into the hot water and carries upon it the cupping instrument until the vessels appear then he pulls above the ankle joint with the company and the vein is in its place at the calf visible towards the thumb.
And it branches out from the face of the leg many branches, so bleed him in the widest branch among them or at the calf when they meet; for that is better and safer. If you bleed him on the face of the leg, he will be saved from the nerves that are under it on the face of the leg.
Make the bleeding appear as if you are cutting him, and let the knife be slack. If it is difficult for the blood to come out, let his leg return to the hot water and leave the blood to run in it until it empties. If the bleeder misses the vein with the first attempt.
He should be evacuated upwards a little, as the place is safe and there will be no fear of complication if he has been preserved from the nerve, just as we said. He should also do the same with the vein on the other leg, whichever one it may be. As for the saphenous vein, its place is as I mentioned it before, on the posterior side.
And the benefit of his evacuation for a hip injury if it was due to hot blood and how to do his evacuation, if you insert a small rod into the artery and hurry and tie down his leg from below the hip up to above the ankle with four fingers thick thread.
If it appears, then evacuate him in any condition; or twist him and that is better; or cut him with a cutting tool or slash him. For his place is safe, and he is very small in most people; if you do not find him.
And if it has not appeared at all, then evacuate some of its branches which are the ones that appear on the back of the foot towards the big toe and the small toe, and preserve from the nerves and send out the quantity of blood you want.
Then untie the ligature and put a lint on the place of the incision and tie the ligature, and it will quickly heal.
The sixtieth chapter.
For cupping and how to use it.
The cups may be made of horns, and wood, and brass, and glass. And the cupping may be on two faces; one of them is the cupping with condition, that is the drawing out of blood, and the other is the cupping without condition.
And this cupping which is without condition will be on two faces.
The supports of the lumbar vertebrae, which are two plates at the back of the neck on both sides, the support of the lower jaw from within the mouth, the supports of the shoulders, the supports of the buttocks below the coccyx, the supports in the middle of the arms, the supports of the legs, and the supports of the ankles.
The venesection draws the blood from the large veins dispersed in the flesh. And therefore, it is not fitting that its force be a throwing out (i.e., of the needle), nor should the venesection be performed with fire or without fire for any of the diseases which result from over-fullness until the body has been completely purged.
If necessity demands venesection because of a disease or in accordance with habit, then we use it at any time, whether at the beginning of the month, its end, or in the middle, or whenever it is needed.
And among people, there are those in whom the blood is abundant to such an extent that they need to have it extracted through venesection, and find a heaviness and headache in their head, fullness and redness on their face, neck, and chest. And some of them experience a buzzing or trembling sensation on their face, groin, and thigh. And there are those who feel darkness and clouding in their eyes. And among them are those who find hardness around the supports (i.e., the places where they have been cupped).
And among them are those whose laughter is excessive, and there are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth, and their gums tremble and bleed. And some of them sleep excessively, and some see in their sleep blood, redness, killing, and wounds, and things similar to that. Whenever we saw something like this,
And especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we are ordered to perform venesection after two or three hours have passed from noon. And as for the benefit of cupping on the nape, it benefits those with heaviness in the head and water that ascends to the eyes.
However, the patient's body should be purged beforehand. This bleeding may serve instead of blood-letting with cupping vessels; and he is warned not to use it from someone who has cold temperament or has a head flux, for it will harm him greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold head afflictions should not use it; nor those who have indulged in it to such an extent that it has caused him forgetfulness.
Therefore, the surgeon should order that his hand be lowered slightly into the cupping vessel below, in fear of causing forgetfulness. And as for knee bleeding, it is a substitute for cupping the eye and letting blood from the temples.
Therefore, it is beneficial for those with asthma and shortness of breath, stridor and coughing, and fullness; and the knee cup should be raised slightly because if lowered too much, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping the hollows of the joints, it is beneficial for those who experience pain in the head, inflammation, tightness, constriction and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for letting blood from the temples. The surgeon should order that his hand not be deeply inserted lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
And as for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for those who have ulcers in the mouth and decay of the gums and similar diseases that are in the mouth; and it may serve instead of letting blood from the cheeks. And as for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for those with palpitations due to fullness and heat.
And as for cupping the hollows of the buttocks, it is beneficial in that it benefits letting blood from the three vessels (of) the temples, the eye and the neck, because it attracts blood from all those subtle vessels which are in the flesh; and it attracts those subtle vessels from thicker vessels until it reaches the thick vessels.
It is advisable that the barber-surgeon should not delve too deeply into the condition, because the area is marked by flesh and beneath it are nerves and veins. And as for a single compress to be applied over the hemorrhoids, it benefits from anal fistulae and lower wounds.
It is advisable that the barber-surgeon should command a compress to be large and made of copper, because the area needs strong pressure. Perhaps the glass compress has broken and many conditions have been imposed upon it. And as for leg bandages, their filling is deficient by clear deficiency.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, womb and bladder and stimulates menstruation and benefits from boils and abscesses and performs the function of letting out the saphenous veins and the ligatures, except that it exhausts the body greatly and a purging occurs in most people. The bandages for the sciatic veins have almost the same benefit as the bandages for the legs.
The manner of placing the bandages is that the compress should be placed first empty and pressed with moderate pressure, without prolonging the placement of the bandages but rather putting them on quickly and taking them off quickly so that the humors return to the place evenly. And you continue this process until you see the area has reddened and opened up.
And the redness of the blood appeared, then he should compress and repeat the pressure gradually and then look at the condition of the bodies. So whoever from people is lean flesh with porous pores it is advisable that he be compressed only once, lest the area become ulcerated.
The barber-surgeon should command him to widen the compress and slightly deepen it, and adjust the pressure gently and softly. If it is in thick blood, he should be compressed twice. In the first instance, he should open a way for the clear blood and its fluidity, and in the second, to expel the thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, we repeat the condition a third time to reach perfection. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, one bandage is enough; but if we wish to remove much blood, we use more bands. If we reckon that the blood is thick.
It should be bandaged deeply with a medium thickness of the cloth, which reaches only the skin. The unguents used for the dressing and the water that is drunk by the patient should not be used; but he who has thick, hard, rough and narrow skin.
It is necessary to apply ointments to the areas of the dressings that are open and dissolved; but if it is in the summer time, like the unguent of roses or the unguent of violets or the sweet almond oil or the juice of colocynth.
And if it is in the winter time, like the unguent of narcissus or the unguent of jasmine or the unguent of lily or like them. And if the ulcer is thick and cold, then let the ointment be the unguent of marshmallow or the unguent of nard or the unguent of saffron or like it.
If the patient has wide pores and a looseness of flesh, it is necessary to abstain from ointment; and these should wash their dressings with rose water or cold water or fox berry juice or colocynth juice or radish juice and like them. And as for him whose blood is very moist.
He washes his dressings with vinegar or with mulberry water and pomegranate juice and like them. And as for him whose ulcer is thick, he washes his dressings with old wine or with marshmallow water or colocynth decoction or jasmine-like waters and like them. It is necessary to beware the bleeding in the bathroom and after the bath.
It is advisable that one should use the steam bath after an hour or two of hmorrhoidectomy, and no one should sleep after it. What the patient who has been cupped and blistered should prepare before and after the operation.
It is necessary to look first; if the patient who has been cupped and blistered has jaundice, with a strong likelihood of bile in his blood, then he should take cooling medicaments such as dates, purslane, khell, honey, and jalab. His diet should be easy like stew, goat meat soup, and sweet dishes similar to them.
And if the patient has a cold temperament, he should be given honey drink and rose water or sweetened jalab. He should eat moderate wine which is between old and new, he should take little food and make his diet of soft dishes such as stew, small birds, and pigeon meat easy to digest. And on the day of hmorrhoidectomy and cupping, he should have more drink than food.
And it is advisable to give him sometimes for some people the antidote faruq or musk medicine or shiltha before hmorrhoidectomy and cupping, or after them, in order to strengthen the main organs and purify the blood. And he should not be given intoxicants.
And as for the cupping without a condition, it is the one that is applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, belly and back, and the place of the kidney and hip because these organs cannot bear bleeding on them. The intention behind it is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
We place the cups on the breasts in case of mastitis or we use them to make a cold sweat come out from the organ that has become hot and congested, just as we place them on the belly and back; it loosens the member, warms it up and takes away the pain by evaporating the bad air.
A pressure is placed on the kidney if a stone or gravel appears in it; perhaps the stone will be drawn out or the gravel will be loosened from its place. And similarly, this pressure is applied to the liver and spleen when there is an uproar in them. This bandage can also be used empty alone with a poultice.
And it may be used with fire, and it may also be used filled with warm water for the cure of hernias; this is that one fills the bandage with very hot water alone or with some herbs which are suitable for that.
Then it is placed filled on the place and held in that state and left, and then repeated as often as necessary. This is the form of the bandage used with fire:.
Its mouth is a span and a half, open as we have shown, and its depth is about the length of half a finger. In the corner of it, in about one-quarter of that, there is a small hole, just big enough to put an awl through, made from thick, yellow tin or Chinese brass.
A flat, smooth, hollow bandage so that it does not harm the part when it is placed on it. In the middle of it there is a metal or iron rod where the candle is placed with fire. And this bandage can be made large, bigger than what we have described, and small according to the diseases.
A cloth is used in its application; the bandages of children and infants are not like those of men. As for how to put this bandage with fire on the part, it is that one takes a thread from cotton well-tied or a small candle from tallow and puts it on the middle of the rod which is fixed in the middle of the bandage.
So that the fire rises up to above, about a span and a half of the bandage, so that it does not burn the body of the person being operated on. Then it is placed on the part and the finger is put on the hole which we mentioned, until the cloth is grasped, then one does not need to remove the finger.
The steam came out through that puncture and the stricture was resolved at the site, then the probe progressed along the ureter and returned if it was needed, but the stricture caused by water in the bladder does not have a retracted rod nor a puncture.
And it is used by filling with water and placing it on the organ only, and this is its form:.
And this stricture was better when it was large so as to hold a lot of water.
The Seventh Part.
For the remedy of the stone in the bladder.
The catheter is used in most cases for the organs which cannot have the stricture placed on them, either because of their smallness like the cheek and the tongue and similar ones, or because the organ is obstructed from flesh like the finger and the nose and similar ones.
And the manner of its use is that you take from the catheter which is in the clear and clean water free from impurities, then leave it for a day and night in the fresh water until it becomes dry and nothing remains inside it. Then the body is first purged by venesection or cupping, then the affected organ is cleansed.
Until it becomes red, then it is placed on it. If it is filled it falls off, and if possible the place is coated with a stricture but if not then the place is washed with vinegar and then with much water, it is rubbed and squeezed, and if the blood flows after the stone has fallen and this was profuse let a linen cloth be soaked in cold water.
And it is placed on top until the bleeding stops, and if the blood flows abundantly ashes of za'ja or alum or similar substances from the soapwort are sprinkled on it until the bleeding stops or a piece of cut papyrus is put on the place.
Leave until the bandage is applied there, as the bleeding will cease. If it is necessary to reapply the ligature, do not remove that ligature if possible; however, if the ligature refuses to be reapplied, clean the area with warm blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until something comes out of the blood, then it is put there. As long as it feels anything from the blood to stick in its place; if you want it to fall, sprinkle some vinegar or salt or ash on it so that it falls onto its place.
The Third Chapter.
In fracture(s).
This chapter is also a trick and necessity for the craft of medicine, which is treating fractures and dislocated bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is also claimed by the knowledgeable physicians and the common people, and by whoever has not reviewed a book on it nor read a single letter from among the ancients.
On account of this reason, this art has been nonexistent in our land and I have not seen it done properly at all. And I have benefited from it only as much as my prolonged reading of the books of the ancients has allowed, and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted its knowledge from them; then I persisted in practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter everything that is contained in my knowledge of it. And my experience has been added to it after I have brought you and simplified it from the branches of complexity, shortened it to the utmost shortening, made clear its indications and depicted for you in it many images of the tools that are used in it.
As is its clarity, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no power but with Allah the Most High the Great.
The First Chapter.
There are some general phrases and sections related to the breaking of bones, which must be presented before we start mentioning the broken and dislocated limbs one by one. In the beginning of this chapter, there should be mentioned some preliminary words and chapters. You are first compelled to understand them, and you must stand firm on the truth of them. And it is not you alone who should be eager to learn this noble profession, but anyone seeking to master this craft.
And I say that whenever one of breaking, dislocation, or fracture, or fall occurs to anyone, it is necessary to rush first to its remedy or treatment or both. If a hindrance such as weakness of power prevents it, or if the one who suffered from it is a child or an elderly man, or if the time is very hot or very cold.
Then he will restrict his food to cold legumes, game meats and cold jadah. He must avoid drinks and thick meats, and be satisfied with little food, so that the vessels do not become filled with blood, even if the swelling has subsided and no liquid is expected to flow into the place.
Then the patient should return to his original treatment. If the broken bone is taken in a splint, then the patient should be fed nourishing foods that are rich, thick and easy to digest, such as barley, wheat bread, heads of cattle and onions and cow's tripe. And eggs, dried fish and thick drinks, and similar things. With this treatment the union of the fracture will be faster and better. And know that the bones which are broken in strong men and elderly people can never reunite and heal naturally as they were originally.
The bones of old men are thin and brittle, and the broken ones will reunite and heal like those of young children. But nature produces on the fractured bone something resembling a glue in thickness that unites and tightens it until they become attached to each other.
And some of them (are) tied to each other, until it comes in a great strength and trustworthiness as it was originally until nothing of its deeds hinders it.
And because of this reason, it is necessary to make the patient's food nourishments which have durability and density as we said, and know that a fracture may differ in its kinds according to the difference of the members because a fracture of the leg bone is contrary to a fracture of the head bone and a fracture of the chest bone is contrary to a fracture of the back.
And likewise all the other members are contrary to one another, and we will come with a mention of every type of fracture described in its place in detail from the others, and the kind of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
Because it may be a fracture which is fractured without causing shavings, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone with shavings and protruding and non-protruding splinters, and there may be a fracture with a wound and an incision in the skin, and there may be a light bruise.
And for every type there is a special trick in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And what indicates a fracture of the bone is its distortion and bending and protrusion to the sense and crunching when you touch it with your hand.
So if there is no obvious distortion, or crunching, or feeling of disturbance in the bone when you touch it, nor does the patient find much pain, then there is no fracture but rather a bruise or a slight crack or a light bruise; therefore do not move him with stretching and pinching at all.
But rather take him on the remedies that will be mentioned afterwards when it suits the place, then pull it with a gentle pull, and know that if the bone is splintered and broken into two without causing shavings, except that all parts have been separated from each other.
You should begin from the time you are able to correct him and straighten him out before a hot tumor occurs in him. If it happens that a hot tumor occurs in him, leave him for some days until the hot tumor settles down then correct him in any way you are able with kindness and cunning.
Know that straightening and correcting him is easier than the bone in which fragments have occurred, then bind on what will be mentioned later. If the bone has fragments, there must be a broken member extended from two hands or a man.
Or with your hands if the member is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. Let it be that you put the member in its natural place until straightened, then when the fractured bone extends, at that time pull back those protrusions to their places in any way you are able with cunning and kindness.
Be careful with your effort so that by your action you do not cause pain or discomfort to the patient. Make an effort to bring one of the bones together with its fellow in the best form. It is necessary at this time for you to touch them with your hand and feel them. If you see something contrary, correct it and straighten it as much as you are able.
Be careful of strong pressure and powerful traction, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause a hot tumor or inflammation in the member as you have seen them doing repeatedly.
Then bind the member after straightening, correction and traction, to rest and calmness. Warn the patient to move it in his wakefulness and sleep, at its change and disturbance, at his urination and all of his movements until he is able, so that it may be a cure for the part that he fears pain with.
So that whenever he feels with his member in its cure position pain or discomfort, he moves it to a different position where he does not feel pain and tries while doing so that the other position be straight and upright.
If the limb becomes numb, deformity will occur in the member. However, as for how to tighten a broken member, it is as I have described to you. Know that fractured members vary in their smallness and largeness and their cleanliness; some are small like the arm and the finger and the hand and similar things.
It is necessary that the bandages of the fracture be soft and thin, but if it is in the large bones such as the thigh and back and chest, then the bandages should be hard and rough. For a wide bandage encases the big member and tightens it evenly from all sides without any unevenness.
Then you start after freeing yourself from the alignment, to apply the plaster suited to that. Then you start to apply gentle pressure on the place of the fracture, then begin to roll the bandage on the place of the fracture itself three or four rolls according to what the member deserves. Then hold your hand a little with the bandage and go with it to the upper side from the place of the fracture.
With a pull less than the pull for the fractured place, then you distance yourself a little with the roll from the place of the fracture and relax the tension a little until it takes something good from the correct place. Then take another bandage and wrap it also on the fractured place.
Rolls, then go with the roll to the lower side from the fracture. Let your action in tightening and relaxing the roll be as we mentioned for the first roll above, then put between the rolls some soft pressure or bandages to equalize the deformity of the fracture.
If there is deformity, then put something in it; otherwise do not put anything in it. Then wrap another bandage over it and straighten up on these firm rolls from your time if there is no swelling or heat in the member.
If there is an inflation or a hot tumor, carry on it what quiets that tumor and gets rid of the inflation, then leave it for some days. Then put on it when the time comes the flasks made from half-twisted reeds cut into shape with skill or the flasks made of palm wood.
The one made from cedar or palm fronds or papyrus or date-palm leaves, similar to what has been prepared of that kind. And let the capacity of each flask be in this shape and form.
Unless the flask that is placed on the break itself is thicker and a little wider than the other flasks, and as for the length of the flask, it should be made according to what suits the broken member in its size, small or large.
Then bind the flasks with another bandage, in accordance with how tightly you bound the first one, then tie from above with strong threads, as we have mentioned that your binding should be on the place of the break more. And however far away from the break was the binding, it was less tight.
It should be that the threads are medium in thickness and softness, and they should be made of wet linen specifically. Because the threads if they were thick like what you have seen from the acts of the foolish people who make their threads from unfolded linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the binding with them goes outside of moderation, and also the soft threads do not suit because you cannot achieve the desired tightness with them. And it should not be less than a finger's width between the flasks.
If the patient is injured by the edges of the flasks after being tied in the proper places, put under them linen or wool padding until nothing hurts him from that. And as for if the break has a wound and there was a hole in the skin, we will attend to it separately.
Know that not every broken member should be bound with splints from the first day, for if the member is large then it should not have splints placed on it unless after five or seven days or more as you deem safe from occurring of hot swelling.
And this is the nature of the plaster which the ancients used to bind with, which is placed on the fracture and jaw and swelling, the nature of a common short binding plaster that consolidates the fracture and suits most tissues especially children and women because it does not cause either heat or cold.
And take the flour dust from between the millstones when they are in motion, mix it as it is without grinding with white eggs and make its dough neither thick nor thin then cook it.
The nature of another plaster that consolidates the fracture and dislocation and swelling, taken from cow's milk, and the root of the plantain and aloe and rasaan and mughath and sakk, each one ten drachmas, and from the root of the marshmallow five drachmas, and from Armenian or Roman clay twenty drachmas. All of them are crushed and mixed with marshmallow water or white egg if the temperament of the patient is disturbed, then this plaster is used because it is good in composition consolidates broken bones quickly and suits most people because of its moderation.
The nature of another plaster that also benefits the fracture and swelling, taken mughath and cow's milk and white ram's horn from each one ten drachmas, mercurial and rasaan from each one five drachmas, aloe six drachmas, Armenian clay twenty drachmas. All are crushed finely and mixed together and kneaded with water or white egg and used.
The nature of a plaster for joints and dislocated bones that go out of their places, and drives away the pain that opposes them and loosens the bone fracture, taken washed wool which is immersed in cooked vinegar and oil, and put on the place, and this plaster does not have consolidating power.
But he is excellent in soothing the hot tumor and relieving pain particularly. A characteristic plaster that forces the broken bone to set, one takes and crushes the inner bark of the terebinth tree and the inner bark of the Nigella plant together and applies them moistened. A characteristic plaster another short used when there is a fracture of the bone.
And you wish to analyze the rest of the tumor, take from the onion and the hibiscus and the petals of the violet and the powder of the cranesbill, crush all together and mix with varnish if the part is not inflamed, then mix with rosewater or water if it is inflamed and use.
A characteristic plaster stronger in analysis than the first one is used when a hard tumor occurs due to bone fracture, it is taken from the onion and the seeds of flaxseed and rue and cloves and mustard and violet petals and hibiscus from each one part.
They are all crushed and mixed with vinegar or sweet water or varnish, all according to the temperature of the part and its quietness, and as for Galen he did not mention that it is put on the fractured bone when it is set.
Except for the wax and oil which are used alone, a description that it should be medium between thickness and softness, and as for Galen he thought that when setting the fractured bone things with skin and something of heat such as resin and myrrh and saffron and what we mentioned should be put on.
A characteristic plaster that is beneficial for weakness and pain, it is taken from gallnut and mustard and human hair or bird feathers and onion and salt equal parts, crush and sift and mix and apply with it, and as for the amount that should remain the bandage.
Then it is peeled off, and look at the place, if it did not cause great pain or crackling or movement of the fractured bone from its place then do not leave it for a long time because if it does occur severe cracking or anxiety or swelling.
Then hasten to apply the poultice at once, and remove the plaster from it. Then take a soft, moist linen cloth or sponge, submerge it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the swelling subsides and the pain lessens. Then let the limb rest for an hour.
Then place on it a swab moistened with vinegar and oil or rose ointment, and tie it up for the night so that the inflammation subsides and the part ceases to swell. Then return it to gentle stretching and light plastering but not as tight as the first time.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, swelling and all symptoms have disappeared altogether and it needed the plaster and stretching as before, then return to do so again in the same way. But if there is nothing wrong with the part as we mentioned.
Do not apply plaster to it except after three, four, five or seven days, and sometimes one can leave it for twenty days, all according to what appears to you of the state of the limb as we mentioned until if the fracture is complicated and the bone approaches being reunited on it.
Also, increase the stretching at that time more than your first pulling of it all. Also, increase also the nourishment for the fractured part on what we have mentioned before regarding it, if you see the site of the fracture has dried up and become worse than it should be, then know that the nourishment is being obstructed from reaching it.
Then wash it with lukewarm water whenever you plaster it again, and let that be every three days. Also ease the pulling a bit because by this action nourishment will reach the part and it will heal quickly. And as for what the ignorant have fabricated from the break of the member occurring another time.
For if it does not heal properly first and becomes crooked, then that is a great mistake on their part. And if what they fabricated was correct, they would have mentioned it at the beginning in their books and implemented it, and I have seen no effect of any of them in this regard whatsoever. The correct thing is not to operate on him.
Chapter Two.
In fractures in the skull.
The types of skull fractures are numerous and varied, and their causes are diverse. Some fractures may result from a blow with a sword, and the bone may be completely shattered until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. As splinters do in wood.
And for that reason this type of fracture is called splintered, and it may be that the sword cuts some of the bone and crushes its surface only, without penetrating to the end, and this type of fracture is simply called crushed. And the wound of both fractures can either be large or small.
And there is a type of fracture which is brittle or crushed, and its cause is a blow with a stone or it has fallen on a stone or the like. This type of fracture can either be that the bone membrane has been almost ruptured or it may be in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small, and there is a type of fracture that is hidden in the bone of the skull hairline and it is a slight impression.
And for that reason this type of fracture is called hairline, and there is a type of fracture that results from a fall or a stone splinter and it enters the layer of bone inside and becomes an ulcer like that which would occur if a blow with lead hit it and part of it entered inside.
And that is more common in the fresh bone skull, like those of young children's heads. And there are fragments of all these types of fractures which are either clean or unclean and we will mention the treatment of all of this in its place, and we will identify all these types of fractures by cutting them open.
And examining them with probes and removing the putrid flesh from them, and as for the hairline type it is identified by cutting open the bone and wiping and staining on it with ink because the fracture becomes black. And as for treating the fracture we look first at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms something that clearly points to fear, such as vomiting of bilious matter and diarrhoea, departure of the mind, loss of voice, delirium, acute fever, and convulsion or redness and bulging of the eyes, then do not approach him nor treat him because death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not frighten you and you hope for his safety, then treat him in this way. That is, if the wounded man comes to you at first when he was injured and it was on a winter's day.
It is necessary to exert effort in removing the bone before the fourteenth day at the very least, and if it was on a summer's day, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, so that what is under the bone does not decay and those symptoms we mentioned will be exposed.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and was accompanied by swelling and inflammation, then you should cut the fractured part that is damaged as I have described to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And you should expose the bone in any way that you are able to, and according to the shape of the wound and what is hidden from the patient, if when exposing the bone you find bleeding or a hot tumour, then treat it as necessary.
And this is that you should stuff the place with soaked cloths in vinegar and oil of red roses, and then sew up the wound until the tumour subsides and bleeding is secured. Then take to consolidating the bone and removing it, which will be on one of two ways of doing things.
As for the one way, that you should cut the bone with a fine toothed saw, and this is its form:.
Then use another saw after this saw, one that is slightly wider than it, and this is its form:.
Then you also use another segment which I have also mentioned, which is obligatory for you to have several segments that are different from each other. Some of them will be longer than others and some will be shorter than others, and they will be very sharp at their ends, made of good Indian steel or good iron.
And use a forceps to strike on the segment, lest it shake the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and hard, you should drill around it before using the segments with the non-piercing reeds which are called non-piercing reeds.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the bone of the skull to what is behind it, in order that the reed has a curved edge below its sharp head similar to a small rope or circle, which prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
You should take a number of these reeds, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone until it prepares you with each skull to be drilled to the length and shortness of the sharp end of that skull. And here is an image of three types. Of the reeds, large, medium, and small:.
And as for the way of drilling around the broken bone, it is that you place the reed on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has perforated, then move the reed to another place and leave a space between each hole the thickness of a reed or near.
Then you cut with the segments what is between each two holes and do that with great care as we said until the bone comes out either by hand or with something else from some of the tools which you have prepared for this.
Such as the silk and the fine calabashes, and you should be careful with all care that neither the reed nor the segment touches anything of the membrane. If you have loosened the bone and freed it from the membrane if it was stuck to it, you should clean it.
The roughness that remains in the bone is smoothed with another instrument similar to a chisel, but finer and softer than all other chisels. If there remain small bones or shreds, they are taken gently with what instruments you have.
Then the wound is treated after that with catgut and ointments which are mentioned, and as for the other aspect of the work it is an easy matter far removed from danger, which Galen has noted and praised greatly. This is what he says. You should first begin by removing a part of the bone from the place where it was broken, the more severe and more common. If you remove that part, then insert the tip of this chisel under it and this is its shape.
The part of the chisel that is rounded out is smooth and does not cut anything, while the sharp part of it on its movable sides as you see, so that the rounded part extends to the peritoneum and the face of the sharp part in the bone. Then strike with the chisel from one direction.
With a small mallet until it cuts all the bone gently, as it turns around and you are safe from the membrane in which no frightening event occurs. And if the healer is one of the most ignorant people and the most clumsy among them yes, even if he was careless.
If there remains something necessary for the bone from the membrane in some places of the bone, then shake it off with the rounded tip of the chisel itself and free him from it gently. For he frees himself from it without harm or fear.
And as for if the bone break had not reached the membrane and what was cut from the bone faced downwards and remained in it some roughness and shreds, then you should strip that roughness and peel those shreds with gentle rasps which have been made of them of different measurements so that you can use them wherever it suits you best and correct them according to the work that guides you.
The shape of the broken bone and its form, it is necessary that you first use in your chest the widest of these splints then use after that finer than it and do not stop doing this until you come to using the finest of them all.
And as for the small, thread-like cracks and fine breaks, it is necessary to treat each one with a treatment according to what will restore its soundness. It is something that will not be hidden from anyone in this craft who has the least bit of knowledge, and we have stopped at what we have written and explained about the large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, it is necessary that you take a linen cloth of the size of the wound and immerse it in rose oil and put it on the mouth of the wound. Then take another folded or triangular cloth. And immerse it in vinegar and rose oil and put it on the first cloth, and do this with as little weight as you can so that you don't burden the omentum. Then use it above a wide bandage and don't pull it tight except for what will hold the wound only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the inflammation subsides. Then cover it and use after that the medicines which are like myrrh, such as senna root, finely powdered gentian, and zedoary and similar to them, by making from these medicines a poultice.
And apply it on the wound as it is dry and in general use for that a medicine whose purpose is to clean and not to become suppurated. And make an effort in treating your wound so that it will be clean, without grease or pus, and do not let the discharge accumulate in it at all.
Because the discharge if it accumulates on the membrane of the brain corrupts and deteriorates it, and a great mischief will happen from that to the patient. And it may expose in the fontanelle of the head when it is uncovered, especially if it is neglected in its treatment.
If the skin on his surface appears black, then consider him doomed, as was previously mentioned. Know that if the blackness occurred due to a medicine that was placed on it and because of the strength of that medicine it caused this blackness.
It is fitting to take a part from honey and three parts of rose oil, then beat them well together. Then smear the mixture onto a cloth and place it on his abdomen, then treat him with all types of treatment until he recovers.
The Third Chapter.
For stopping a nosebleed if it is broken.
Know that the upper part of the nasal passage does not break, rather one or both of them break in order for it to be a fracture. For below is cartilaginous and does not break but rather becomes swollen, discolored and ulcerated.
If one of the upper parts of his nasal passage breaks, it is fitting to insert a small finger into the nostril and straighten that break from inside with your index and thumb fingers from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently and be careful not to cause harm or pain to him.
If the break is in the upper part of his nasal passage and you do not insert a finger into it, then he should be straightened with the edge of a spoon that is slightly thick. If the break is on both sides, then do the same. And he should begin to treat it from the first day of the break.
If possible, and otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the inflammation has subsided, then insert a thread of linen cloth into the nostril. If the break is on one side, insert one thread, and if it is on both sides, insert two threads.
And let the thread be thick in accordance with what fills the nostril. And some early physicians have said to soak the thread in lard and change it every day, but I do not see that as correct, rather it should be soaked in white egg yolk mixed with saffron powder.
Then leave the bandage until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens. And for the nasal septum, the place of the bandage may be inserted tubes from the feathers of a goose after they have been wrapped with a linen cloth so that their confinement may cause a harder break in the nose lest the patient should not breathe properly.
And this is not a necessary thing if you wish to make it or if you wish to make the bandages, for the tip of the nose in your work becomes swollen and hot, so bind the nose with lint or soaked cotton dipped in rose oil or fat or something from the paste of the Diachylon.
If the swelling does not appear, then bind it from outside with fine semolina and kender powder which has been kneaded with the white of egg, then place a linen compress on top and do not tie the nose with anything whatever, if the bones of the nose are broken in small fragments.
Or if it is fractured, then make an incision on it and take it out with the instrument that is suitable for that, then sew up the cut and treat it with what unites and heals from the appropriate pastes. If there was a wound inside the nose, then treat it with the bandage and use lead tubes. Until he recovers.
The Fourth Chapter.
For the treatment of a broken upper lip.
If the lower lip is broken and there is no wound, if it is a break from outside only and has not been split into two and is cracked inside then knowing it makes it easier.
If the break is in the right fissure, then insert the left forefinger into the patient's mouth and also if the break is in the left fissure, then insert the right forefinger and raise the protrusion of the break from inside to outside gently.
And with the other hand from outside the bone, control it so that it is smoothed out. If the jaw fracture has split into two parts, then use a spatula on both sides until it can be smoothed out. If there was a growth in the teeth, it should be treated accordingly.
If it is broken, fasten what you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or wire, then place the ligatures on the fractured jawbone. Then place over it a folded cloth and place on the cloth a large bandage or a piece of leather sole equal in length to the jawbone. Then tie it from above in the way that seems suitable to you, so that he is tight and snug. Order the patient to remain still and be quiet, and make his food a soft diet. If you think that something has changed in his shape on one of its sides, take action with him on the third day.
Then tie it from above in the way that seems suitable to you, so that he is tight and snug. Order the patient to remain still and be quiet, and make his food a soft diet. If you think that something has changed in his shape on one of its sides, take action with him on the third day. Then repair what you have packed with the emery powder mixed with egg whites or flour after removing the ligatures from it. Place over the poultice a soft compress, as long as it sticks to that poultice and does not change for bone, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until it heals and the fracture gets stronger. Often this break becomes strong in three weeks, if an abscess appears during that time, make use of what we have mentioned repeatedly to alleviate its swelling until it disappears.
And as for if the break is accompanied by an ulcer, then if a shard or shreds of bone have healed out of it, smooth its removal with what agrees to you in removing them from the instrument. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with the probe according to your need.
Then, if you have removed those shreds and there is nothing left of them, sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; otherwise apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And close the wound until it heals.
The Fifth Chapter.
For setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus more often fractures in front of the shoulder from its end and its break is on one of three faces, either that it shatters into two pieces without there being shards in it, which is easier to set.
If the break is shattered and that is more difficult to treat; or if it is with a wound, you should act as follows: If the break is not with a wound, have two servants ready, one to steady the forearm near the broken arm, and the other to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then you should adjust the break with your fingers until its shape becomes as it should be, and there should not be a swelling nor a bruise in it. If you needed to pull more, it is necessary to place under the elbow of the patient a ball made from wool or flax.
And its bone will be according to your need, you should extend and lift the forearm, and press with your hand on the ball until the break is adjusted as it should be. If you were not able to pull the tip of the forearm outside because it had sunk in deeply, then the patient should lie on his back.
And a folded cloth should be placed under his armpit in the bone, and the servant should press on his armpit downwards until the bone of the forearm that is deep inside is raised upwards. Then you may correct the break and straighten it with your fingers.
If you felt that a fragment of the forearm had broken and was moving, then it is necessary to cut through it and remove that fragment gently. If the fragment was embedded in the bone, then take one of the instruments prepared for this purpose and use it to cut it. After the apparatus that protects the membrane has been placed under the forearm, which is an instrument made of wood or iron, and this is its form:
It resembles a spoon with no flaw, and its width should be according to what you need of the bone's size and smallness. As for its length, it should also be according to what you can do with it. And it should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower.
If the wound cut that you made when removing a splinter of bone is wide and you have prevented the suppurating swelling, then sew up both sides of the wound with stitching. And if the cut is slight or you are afraid of the suppurating swelling, then reopen the wound by cutting it again and use a dressing according to the width of the cut wound.
If a suppurating swelling appears, then scrape it before cutting with kohl or vinegar wine or drink. And if the bone is set without cut nor incision, then apply to the bone a plaster made from ground pumice mixed with white of egg. And place a soft, folded pad on it. Then put the ball under his armpit if necessary. Then take a long turban, its width being about a forearm or somewhat more than that. Then put a folded dressing on the pad and the plaster. Then take a thin wooden splint. Its width and length should be three fingers. Then wrap it in a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the place of the turban where the fracture is located. Then tie the turban on the break as one wraps a bandage. And tie it around his neck and under his healthy arm and the sick one's. Fold it over on itself several times in each direction. And how you see that the tension corrects the break strongly, which is not hidden from you.
And the condition for all of this is that the splint does not come off the broken bone. And for that reason the patient should be inspected every day. Every time you see the bandage has relaxed and the splint has come off, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then have the patient sleep on his back and under his armpit at night a small pillow.
So that the rib rises with it, and the fracture of the ulna is raised to the height of the shoulder or his arm is tied to his neck. And do not remove the bandage unless an accident occurs such as a wound or swelling in the place within twelve days.
Then renew the poultice if you see fit to do so. It is applied to the swelling and left until it heals and becomes more firm, stronger still in twenty-eight days. And there may be some people who heal in less than that.
Chapter Six.
For the healing of a fractured shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks in its broad place and it usually breaks at its joints, so when it breaks in any other place or breaks in the middle and this is known by touch, then the shape of the break will be as follows.
You mend it and apply to its natural shape with all the devices you can from every side, then carry some powdered pumice mixed with white egg and fine lint over it, and place on top a wide splint of wood.
Thin, as much as the whole shoulder or a little wider than it, if there is swelling under the splint in any of the places of the shoulder, then mend that swelling with fine lint until the splint lies flat, and then bind from above with a long bandage.
Bind firmly and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place, and examine the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it again and mend the splint if it moves from its place. May the swelling on the true side be at rest, and the shoulder heal in twenty days.
Or in five and twenty days, if you have completed this number then loosen the bandage and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones which are not feared to be shattered or broken. If a fragment emerged from the bone and was hidden under the skin, cut into it and extract it. And make what I mentioned in a fractured elbow regarding soothing the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
The Seventh Section.
For healing of a broken chest.
The chest may break in its middle and this is rarely shown, but rather its edges are more likely to break apart. And from its symptoms if it breaks in the middle that it leans down and shows severe pain and difficulty breathing and coughing.
And it may be that blood flows and the separation in the broken bone is evident and not hidden to the touch. And its treatment is for the patient to lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders then compress their vertebrae together with the hands from both sides and calm them as much as possible by laying them flat on any face that can be done.
And when its form returns as it should, then apply a compress and bandage on top of it. And place over that a splint made from a flat piece of willow or mulberry wood or the like in softness after having wrapped it in a cloth. Then calm down in tying it on the broken bone so that it does not move, and make a knot at the binding which turns towards the back a few times and tighten it tightly. Then inspect the bandage at all times, and whenever it relaxes tighten it again.
And if necessity demands its removal from the place or pain or swelling then hasten to remove it, lift off the compress and correct what appeared in that spot by way of treatment then put back the compress if you see a need for it. And face him and order him to tighten until he is healed.
The Eighth Section.
For healing of the ribs if they have broken, know that the breaking in the ribs occurs in the sharp places which follow the back and their edges from the front are exposed to bruises because they are cartilaginous; and knowing this is not hidden to the touch when searched with the fingers.
It is necessary to arrange the fracture by hand on the powerful side until the shape becomes equal to what is required. Then, wet and tighten the broken bone with a splint if it requires that. If the rib fracture tilts inward, it exposes the patient to severe pain.
And the trachea, like the trachea of one who has a goiter, in order for the bone to be visible and exposed. It also causes difficulty breathing and coughing and spitting up much blood. This is a difficult condition to treat. The earlier ones have dealt with it by many tricks.
Some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient food that will cause gas and flatulence so that the stomach swells and the fracture protrudes outward. We dislike this lest it confirm the occurrence of inflammation if there was no inflammation, but if it had occurred then it increases and confirms it.
And some of them said that a warm compress should be placed on the place and squeezed strongly, it is similar to an application but one fears that the compress will attract dirt to the place due to its weakness. And some of them said that the place should be covered with wool that has been dipped in hot oil.
A poultice is made between the ribs until it is full, then the bandage becomes even if one folds it round. Then the patient is treated with the treatment of goiter from food and medicine. If the patient is exhausted, his condition is very severe and he cannot bear it. And the bone would grow into the goiter in an injurious manner and we were worried for the patient, so it is necessary to make an incision on the place and reveal the broken rib then sew under it the apparatus which preserves the membrane that I mentioned and cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we join the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and treat him with ointments until he heals. If inflammation occurs during that time, act beforehand by applying a poultice of rose water on the place and treating the patient with what will calm the inflammation from within as well.
He lies on the side which is furthest from his sleep until he becomes well.
The Eighth Chapter.
For the treatment of scabies of the back and neck.
But as for the bones of the neck, if it has a fracture and this rarely occurs, but more often a boil appears there, and also the lumbar vertebrae, then if one is exposed to that and you wanted to know whether he will get better or not. Then look, for if you see that his hands are stiff and numb and he is not able to move them or straighten them or clench them, and even if you punctured them or scratched them with a needle he felt nothing and did not find any pain in them, then know that in most cases he will not get better, so he is lost.
And if he is able to move them and feels the puncture and scratching in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has escaped and the illness will be cured with treatment. And if the scabies of the back was like that and you wanted to know whether it too would get better or not.
Then look at his legs, for if you see that they are stiff and there has occurred in them what occurred in the hands, then if he lies on his back, wind and stool will come out without his will, and if he lies on his abdomen, urine will come out without his will. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate but is unable to do so, then know that he is lost, so do not concern yourself with treating him. And if nothing of this occurs, the matter will be lighter. The treatment for what occurred from this is to strive to calm the hot abscess.
To put on the affected vertebrae rosewater alone or with cooked egg yolks, and to place it on that three times a day until the hot abscess is calmed down. Then take a padded pad and tie it tightly around the place and order him to remain still and quiet.
A patient should not sleep unless he places the affected side away from his pillow, so that he may rest well. If something has happened to him in the bone at the site of an incision, a splinter or some other object will have formed there; it is fitting that this skin be cut open and that splinter removed. Then the edges of the wound, if it is large, are sewn together with a suture. Then it is treated with ointments until it heals. If the lower bone of the pelvis breaks, which is known as the ischium, then the tip of the left thumb must be inserted into the socket.
The broken bone is set with the other hand, as far as it is possible to do so. Then splints are applied and a bandage wrapped over them if necessary, before pulling tight.
If you feel a splinter that is broken off within it, then cut into it and remove it. And treat the wound with the treatment mentioned above until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
In Setting a Hip Fracture.
Say what breaks the bones of the hip. If they break, then their fracture is that it becomes splintered in its ends and cracked in length and tends towards the inside and exposes itself to danger and pain in that place and swelling and numbness in the leg which was broken from its side.
And set it by passing your hand over it until you stand on the fracture and see what its shape is. If the break is only in its ends, then set that break as you are able to do so with the setting until it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is in length or it has tilted towards the inside, then lay the splint on his stomach until you are able to set that break. If you have set it, then place a splint made of wood or skin over it and pull it tight.
Do not be afraid of the fracture's progression or the dehiscence of the wound and filling of its edges with what fills it until tension is equalized. Command the patient to sleep on his back or sound side, if he has a hot swelling.
Withdraw from him and stitch it until the hot swelling subsides, then put on top of him what will calm it as mentioned before. Then go back to stitching and tightening it properly, if there is a splinter or something has come off from its edges do not remove it.
Nor should he touch it but rather have it sutured from the outside as we said, and leave the tightening until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
In stitching a fracture of the arm.
The arm is what is between the elbow and the top of the shoulder, if it has fractured then stitch it on one of two ways. One is to take a rod that is hollowed out smooth and medium thickness in this shape:
and ties two cords on its ends, then hangs it from a high place. The surgeon sits on a chair, then lets the broken arm hang on the rod until his elbow is pressed against the middle of the rod's curvature, then hangs something heavy above it.
or has it brought down by a servant, then the doctor stitches the fracture with his hands together until the break is returned to its proper place. The other way is for the surgeon to lie on his chest and hang his hand from his neck with a cord.
Then commands two servants to adjust one above the fracture with his hands, and the other below it, and have each one stretch in their direction. And if you want the pull to be stronger, tie a cord under the break and another on top of it, and have both servants stretch them towards theirs.
If the fracture is close to the angle of the arm, it should be wrapped with a bandage under the armpit and the other one near the fracture towards the elbow. Likewise if the fracture is close to the elbow, the bandage should be placed on that spot and also on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture should be aligned gently without violence until the fracture is properly aligned and a good union results. Then it should be tightened, if there isn't an acute swelling, otherwise leave it till the seventh day and put on a woolen compress soaked with vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then it should be tightened. The method of tightening is that a pad is placed on the fracture, then a sheet of fresh cloth is wrapped over the pad, and the arm is brought to rest against the shoulder itself and his hand is placed open on the angle.
And the cloth and the bandage are worn on the shoulder and arm so that the arm acts as a substitute for the splints, unless you are prevented from doing so by something or there is some change in the fractured bone. If you fear there will be some change in that, use splints.
And it is that a splint is placed on the fracture itself, which is wider and stronger than all other splints. And let there be three fingerbreadths between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then tighten over the splints the bandage that was mentioned earlier.
So that your tightness on the place of the fracture is stronger.
And whatever was left after the casting of the splints, if you see that the tightness is less, then place them as we said at the time of casting in your hour.
And if you fear an acute swelling, then leave the tightness and splints until the seventh day as we said.
