Then check the bandage every three days so that a hardness or inflation does not occur in the place, or that food cannot reach the member due to excessive tightness, and correct all of this according to what we mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of this sort will occur, the binding should not be done until a number of days have passed, and the swelling of the wound will be on its back with its hand on its belly and is placed under the elbow muffled filled with wool moderate.
And you must check at all times, day or night, so that the shape of the broken limb does not become distorted or the binding loosen. Correct everything with your effort and make the food of the patient in the order we mentioned, that is, the food should be light first until if the bone has a strong tendency.
It is necessary that his food be thickened, for it is the habit of the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days. Therefore, when this happens, it is necessary that he be bound again and bathed and treated with ointments suitable for that, if the break is very deep and settled.
Do not remove the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
The twelfth chapter.
In setting a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called the humeri, one being small, which follows the wrist, and the other being large, which is located under the small one from below. It may be that only the larger humerus has broken or only the smaller one has broken, or both have broken together.
If the small humerus above has broken, then setting it is easy and its cure will be faster. But if the lower humerus has broken, its fracture is worse and its cure is harder. And it is most difficult to treat them together, when both the humeri have broken, if indeed the humerus of the arm that broke the small one above was in a bad state of health to start with.
It is necessary for the doctor to make its length moderate and gentle so that he can straighten it. If the larger humerus has broken, it is necessary for him to make its length stronger. And if both humeri have broken, it is necessary for him to make the splint much stronger.
It is necessary that the hand be placed in a moulded shape at the time of plastering and stretched out on a pillow, with the tip of the thumb above all the fingers. The little finger should be below all the fingers, and the ring finger raised up high on itself.
And let there be a pillow to his side at this height, so that the ring finger does not have trouble.
Then a servant will extend the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant will extend it from above as well. Then the physician will shape the bone until he returns it to its best shape possible. If there are fragments of bone in the fracture, he will reduce them.
Reduce every fragment to its place with effort. If a fragmented piece appeared that was inflamed and had penetrated the skin, and you have no intention of reducing it, then split it open and extract it in the manner we mentioned earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by an injury, you will have made a cut for him.
Then take treatment from there. If, in your first plastering, a hot swelling appeared, then make a poultice with the mixture of red rosewater and white wax being neither too thick nor too thin, and apply it to him tightly until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the powder and put the prepared plaster with white flour, then take the plasters and let the one that is placed on the fracture itself be slightly larger and stronger. Know that there are usually six plasters for the arm.
If the fracture was in one of the humeral bones or in both together, then make your traction stronger and firmer at the place of the fracture. And whenever you turn with traction upwards or downwards, make the tension looser slightly as mentioned earlier in the first chapter.
And let the poultice that is wrapped over the fracture be a soft, moist linen poultice, not too stiff at all. Let the thread which binds it be from special cotton, being moderately thick, as we have described. And inspect the limb and bandage after some days.
If something happens that requires repair, such as a cut in the member, then the member should be immersed in warm water until the cut is absorbed, and it should not be drawn tight for a night to rest. Then tighten again.
If the tension has relaxed and the bone has been lost or something similar, then you must try to repair all of that with effort. Also, if food refuses to reach the member due to excessive tension, it is necessary for you to loosen it a little and leave it for some days until food reaches it, then tighten it again.
If something that has been mentioned does not become exposed to the open air, it should not be healed except after twenty days or so. Then hang the bandage of the healer onto his neck and let his arm be moderate and keep control over his movements and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm is healed in thirty days or two and thirty, and it may be healed in twenty eight days, all according to the conditions of temperament and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
For healing a break in the palm of the hand and fingers.
The palm of the hand and joints of the fingers are rarely exposed to a break, but rather they are often exposed to an ulcer. When a break or ulcer is shown in the palm,
It should be that the healer sits elevated and before him there is a stool on level.
Then he puts his hand on it, stretched out, then the servant brings the broken bones and the doctor arranges them until if they are set in a good consolidation, then it is necessary to apply a splint and bandage. If there is no hot swelling, then a wooden splint is carried over according to the place.
And I have placed it in a green cloth, if the break is towards the bottom towards the palm of the hand, make the shape of a ball from the cloth and order the healer to grasp it with his broken palm then tie with a long cloth.
Let the cast be made of soft leather so that the skin is moulded with the flexure of all the palm and attached to what is necessary. If the fracture is outside, then make a cast from above and another from below in the palm, so that the hand is open and standing upright.
Then carry the strain as the hand rotates and tie it between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture is in one of the metacarpal bones, then smooth out the thumb as necessary and tie it along with the palm. And if you wish to make for it a small upright cast to set the fracture.
And do not let it move. If the fracture is in one of the other fingers such as the middle, index or little finger then smooth them out and tie with the next sound finger or tie all together to the splint it is better or place a small upright cast on it.
As we said about the thumb, and check with your cast and apart from the warm swelling when it happens as it has been repeated in its description.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
For a splint to set a fracture of the thigh.
The femur often breaks and is felt because it swings forward and backward, and its splint is by tying a bandage above the break and another below the break with the patient lying on his back.
Then extend each slave band to its place with moderation, if the break is in the middle of the bone. And if it is near the hip joint then you should tie a soft band either from wool or something similar.
When the break is near the hip to about the groin, then the extension should be upward and the other band below the break. Similarly if the break is near the knee, then the band should be near the knee so that the extension is downward. Then the doctor smooths out the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape.
When the bone becomes inflamed, its union will be good. In that case, it is necessary to leave the poultice and bandage if a warm swelling develops on the member; for if this happens, wait until the warm swelling subsides, then return to treating it.
And as for the bandage, it should be twisted around the fracture two or three times and a little bit left in place, then the limb should be twisted until the heel is at the level of the ankle. A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below and its ends should protrude from above on both sides.
Then turn the remaining part of the bandage around the thigh and leg, then make small splints on the thigh in place of the fracture itself. Make one splint on the bone of the leg, then fill in the gap between the thigh and leg with soft papyrus to even out the tension.
Then start with a strong binding from the middle on place of the fracture three turns or four, and as you bind the bandage from the place of the fracture let the tension be less and softer each time.
Then take the ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie them to what comes next from the splints above. Then pass through the ends of the thread downwards until they reach the ankle bone, and bind both ends of the splints with it on the other side as well.
So that the bandage does not come off its place. Then leave the tension on it as long as there is no pain, swelling, or inflammation in the member; if something like this happens, take action to resolve it and correct everything as we have mentioned many times.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, it should be straightened out if possible; otherwise cut over it and remove it, then treat the wound as has been mentioned previously until it heals. This fracture may be fixed from the thigh without adding the leg with splints.
As we have mentioned with regard to the arm and forearm, except that when it is fractured so badly as to expose bone, and if only the arm itself was broken, without any part of the leg being attached to it, then it must be permanently elevated.
Know that the thigh takes about fifty days or a bit more to heal, or a bit less, all of this according to the difference in constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
The fracture of the femur.
The femur is rarely exposed to fractures, but it may be frequently exposed to lacerations. If it is exposed to a fracture, it will either break or split into its parts, and this can occur with or without an accompanying wound.
She will be aware of this by the sense, and she will fix it by gathering with her fingers what has been broken into parts until they come together and adhere as much as possible by being put in order, comforted and done accurately. Then she will place the compress on it and place a rolled up splint on it if necessary.
She will tighten the string above from which it was adjusted, then she will take care of all the conditions that we have mentioned in other fractures, such as the hot boil and similar ones by confronting each opponent with what is suitable for it until it clears up.
Chapter Sixteen.
The treatment of a fractured leg.
There are two bones in the leg, one of them is thick and called the bone of the leg, and the other is thin and called the fibula, and it is exposed to types of fractures that occur in the humerus. And because of that, its treatment is the same as the treatment of the arm, whether it be one or the other. If both bones break together, the leg turns in all directions, and if the thin bone breaks, the leg turns forward, and if the thick bone breaks from below, this should not be hidden from you.
Use the splint and bandage, and tie the femur as needed, except that if the tibia is broken with many fragments, use a lighter and less tight splint, and exert yourself in binding it.
In the leg, there is a thing added to the arm's work, that if you have tied the femur and finished all your work, then take two wooden splints from the cedar tree which are used for flattening between cracks of boards or like them.
Choose from them what has a slight thickness and is not too thin, let its length be the same as the leg from the knee down, then wrap on each one two linen cloths along its length, with one cloth placed on the front of the leg and the other on the back.
Let its length be from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two splints in three places at the sides and middle, so that with this hold the leg will not incline to the right or left nor bend well. And it may be used with a board of wood along the leg.
And it is placed in there to preserve him from movement, and more often than not this should be done if there is a special wound with the break, then examine the leg every day and attend carefully to any swelling or inflation or anything else. So when something of that happens, deal with it as it is needed until he recovers, and the leg's bone heals in thirty days or around that.
Chapter Seventeen.
In fractures of the feet and toes.
As for the heel, it is not exposed to fracture at all, and as for the foot bones, they may be exposed to fracture. And also the fingers are rarely exposed to fracture, but rather to laceration in most cases.
If he is exposed to a fracture of the foot bones and you have seen that some of those bones are overlapping each other, then let the patient stand with his foot on the ground upright like walking, then come and place your own foot on what has risen from those bones, and tread upon it and bind it.
Until they return to their places, then take the dressing and bandage from above and place under the sole of the foot a small flat board, then pull with it the foot with great force after loosening for her with the poultice and all that she needs.
If three or four days have passed for him, then release the bandage; you will find that the bones are straight, whether they were broken or dislocated. The treatment is as you see it. And if some of the fingers have been fractured, set and splint them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then place a splint for the fractured finger along its length, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place under the foot this board which I described to you and pull it with great force, if two or three or more of the bones were broken.
So place a splint for each finger made of its equal length in a linen cloth, and pull the board down at the foot so that each end of it is protruding outside the sole of the foot in good adjustment.
And it is incumbent on you not to forget that you should treat all what we mentioned to you in other fractures from the symptoms which we mentioned and meet every symptom with what is appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
In fracture of the thigh of a woman and the bone of the vulva and the penis of a man.
When the vagina of a woman is fractured, place her on high supports then bend her slightly towards her back with firm hold, then fill her vagina with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her vagina.
Then shake the woman and raise her pelvis slightly, since that cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina and the bone fracture returns. Then place a pad on her back. Whenever she wanted to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
If a urine fistula occurs, it is treated as I have mentioned first. Then it returns to its original position, doing so for seven days or so until it heals. If you wish to take out an abscess, tie one end of a short tube onto the mouth of the abscess and insert the whole thing into the vagina.
Then inflate the tube with force until the bladder swells inside the vagina and the fracture returns. Then bind it with cloth and keep for days as we have described until it heals. And if the bone of the penis or the woman breaks, it is used in its treatment.
And its treatment is as we have described for the hip bone. And do not conceal from you that this fracture is rare and especially in one who has some weakness, and my writing of this is a benefit to you because the fracture itself guides you most of the time.
Follow the right treatment in setting and binding it, so you will understand. And if a man's penis breaks, it is put into a bird's stomach then a cloth is placed on it, it is bandaged and left for three days or so until it heals.
The nineteenth chapter.
In treating fractures of the bones if they are accompanied by an injury.
It is necessary for one who presents a fracture with an injury, especially if the bone is large such as the femur or arm or similar to it, to hasten and clean it of its purulent matter if you have the conditions of bleeding as we have mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding with blood, it is necessary to hasten and stop its bleeding by sprinkling powdered alum on it if that is not available then take care of setting the fracture in that day itself without delaying.
If there is no severe inflammation, then set the fracture. But if a severe inflammation occurs, leave setting it until the ninth day so that the inflammation settles and do not approach it on the third or fourth day at all because it will be exposed to bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone protrudes on the skin exposed, it should be sought to return and smooth out with your hand gently and a little stretch. And if you do not come to return and smooth out with your hands, let it be returned by this instrument which is made of iron as long as seven fingers or eight. and expose it on the level of the wound.
Therefore, it is advisable for the physician to take three or four of them as needed for each type of fracture and let them be curved so that they are slightly thick so that they do not bend on her when striking her at the time of work.
And it will be sharp in its end with an angle on one end and the top of it to thickness and from half of it downwards very thin and this is its shape:.
And it is called in Greek Pyrmy which means small wedge, so its sharp pointed end should be placed on the protruding end of the bone and pushed by it once until the bone returns to a certain level of smoothness and the edges of the fracture are smoothed out against each other.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument did not take a good grip on it, then the tip of that bone should be cut off until the instrument can grasp it. And if you were unable to return the bone as we described, cut it with the parts we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the pincers if you can, then strip what remains in the bone from roughness and thick scales. And if after returning the bone you find a severe and painful swelling, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to return it to its natural place, then do so, for you will be of great benefit to the swelling. And if your treatment of the bone is complete, dip a cloth in blackish tightening liquid, especially in summer, and do not put any grease or anything on the wound.
Do not let moisture accumulate in it, and then use the calabashes at a time when you have removed the bone fragments and left the wound open to dry by folding the cloths into a thickness according to the size of the wound.
Be very careful not to close the wound with the fracture, for many foolish doctors have done that and caused their patients either death or gangrene or putrefaction; whereas your closing should be like a linen cloth stretched over it.
If the wound is foul or large and you fear some of the bad signs we have mentioned, and he feels pain in the place disturbed, then do not put calabashes on it; instead make a cloth bandage of strong linen in the place of the calabashes and tie him with it.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to form pus, then remove from him the bandage with which you had moistened him; and then use the poultices and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as the four-cornered ointment and similar ones, and it is necessary for you to replace the bandage.
And check the wound every day at night and morning until it closes and heals; and it is necessary for you to position the part so that pus can flow out from it downwards easily. If many days pass over the wound without its healing or the pus flowing from it ceasing, then know that there are small bone fragments.
And it is necessary for you to search the wound with a probe; what is from those fragments that are not purulent, then extract them and remove them; and what is from them that is not purulent and eats away at the part and causes pain, then crush them and take them out in whatever pieces you can.
If a fever, gangrene or another kind of decay and corruption appears on the wound, then face each one of them with what they require from treatment which we have described in its place.
And it is fitting that you stand by his nose and direct your mind to him if a large bone breaks, such as the femur or humerus, and its protruding part touches the limb. Do not expose yourself to pulling on it nor taking it out. And often from that is presented the threat of death, so leave it until it becomes gangrenous; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
So when that happens, you treat the wound if you see in it a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter twenty.
In the treatment of the complication that occurs in the aftermath of some fractures.
This complication often occurs in the aftermath of setting a fracture, especially when it is near a joint, and it makes the limb ugly; perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Observe, if the complication is soft, then use medications that are applied such as aloes, myrrh, galbanum, onos, and aqua colocynthidis, taking some of them or all of them and mixing them with a binding drink or egg whites or vinegar.
And you apply it to the complication with a bandage and tie it firmly on top of it, leaving the tension for many days and then relieving it, repeating this process until the complication goes away, God willing; or else cover it with a well-finished lead plate.
For lead has a special quality that removes everything in the limbs; if the complication becomes hardened and the necessity compels you to remove it, cut into it from above and cut out or scrape away the resulting cavity with some forceps until it goes away and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter twenty-one.
In the treatment of a fracture if it becomes brittle and the limb remains fragile unlike its original nature.
If the bones have been set and the limb has remained thin and weak afterwards, it is for many reasons. One of them is perhaps because the ligaments were loosened too much and tied in a way that was not fitting, or because they were tightened excessively so that nourishment could not reach the member, or because excessive flexing was done at the wrong time, or because there was a lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
And the treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until blood increases in it, then to use a warm bath and to put joy on him and happiness and similar things. Then to apply poultice on the member so that it attracts much food to it or to keep flexing with lukewarm water. until nourishment is brought into it and it returns to its natural shape.
The 22nd Chapter.
In the treatment of the broken bones if they have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning as they should.
When a member that was set after being crooked, or swollen with the broken bone, or complicated and deformed as a result of it, is presented. Unless the member had not been prevented from performing its natural function, it is not permissible to accept the claim of someone who says that the member should be broken again, and indeed many foolish doctors and practitioners used to do this in our country.
And this action is extremely blameworthy and leads to great chaos, but if the crookedness and complication are slight, it should be treated with water in which the tender shoots of plants such as reeds, its roots and crown have been boiled.
And it is plastered with the softened poultices such as the hardened diachylon or taken from the saliva of reeds and beaten with chicken fat and shiraj fat, then applied, or taken the ripe grape and pounded with pigeon dung and similar medicines that are called dissolvers of congealment.
The complicated knot is loosened with a thin, fine, constant soap which is in the hand. One moves the limb to all directions at times; if deformity appeared and strengthened and hardened and necessity demanded its treatment with iron.
It is necessary that the upper part be cut and the connection of the bone released, and what remained of the knot or the bone be cut with gentle strips. And one should apply softness in that with effort and care, then treat the wound as mentioned above until it heals.
The 23rd Chapter.
The jawbone is a dislocation of the joint from its place, preventing it from moving and deforming the shape of the member, causing severe pain and suffering. When one of them appears, it should be hastened to return it to its place.
He should not delay it at all, for if he delays the place becomes swollen and inflamed, making it difficult to return the jawbone to its place. Therefore, it should not be delayed or moved or extended while it is inflamed because severe pain often occurs.
But if that appears, one should hasten to lance the abscess then leave it until the swelling subsides slightly. Then one bathes the member with hot water and fat, then returns it gently and treats each part as mentioned in its place.
And I have also arranged the chapters of the jawbone in accordance with what was mentioned previously about the fracture, from top to bottom.
The 24th Chapter.
In the treatment of the lower jawbone.
The jawbones are rarely dislodged, and their removal is either partial or complete. Either they slip out of place slightly and are loose a little, or they completely dislodge until they become loose. To the chest, until saliva flows from his mouth and he cannot hold it in or bring himself to speak; as for if his dislocation is slight, he will most often return to himself by an easier means.
As for if the dislocation is complete, it should be hastened and not delayed; when a servant holds the head of the suffering person and the doctor inserts the tip of one finger into the jawbone within his mouth from one side.
Or he inserts the tips of both fingers if the jawbone is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers arrange it from outside; and commands the suffering person to loosen and release his jawbone in all directions, and the doctor arranges the jawbone and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If his return is difficult, especially if both jawbones are dislocated, use the poultice with hot water and oil until it becomes easy to return them; do not delay their return as we have said. When they have returned and settled down and the mouth of the suffering person has closed without spasming, then place on them a compress made from beeswax, oil and red ochre which is made ready.
Then tie him gently with a relaxed cord; and the suffering person sleeps on his back, his head nestled between two pillows so that he will not be moved to the right or left, nor will he have difficulty chewing anything; rather, make his food liquid.
Until the pain has gone and the jawbone has become stable, he should eat whatever he desires; and use it gently without forcing his mouth open when eating, drinking or yawning until the jawbone becomes stable and is healed.
If the return of the jawbones is difficult when they have come loose at some time and have not returned to their places, often as a result of this, fevers and constant headaches will occur and sometimes the belly of the suffering person will go out or he will vomit repeatedly; so if you see that, know that it has been damaged.
He often dies from that condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-five.
For the treatment of a broken shoulder blade and the tip of the scapula.
As for the scapula, it does not break from within, as it is connected to the chest. It breaks outward and this will be apparent by feeling. Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back and stretches out his arms.
Then you press the place with your hand with great pressure until it returns, then put a poultice and a compress on it and bind it. As for its tip which is adjacent to the scapula and connected to it, it does not break except rarely; if it breaks one day then it should be replaced and smoothed out as we have mentioned and what is prepared for you.
Then you put a poultice and a compress on it and bind it, and order the patient to keep still and calm until he recovers. And with this treatment in itself the tip of the scapula will also return if it has moved from its place.
Chapter Twenty-six.
For the treatment of a broken scapula.
Know that the scapula breaks in three ways: one is that it breaks towards the armpit downwards, and the second is that it breaks towards the chest perhaps breaking above the scapula, which is rare.
It does not break towards the back behind the location of the shoulder blade nor does it break forward in front of the nerve, and more often it breaks and comes out downwards towards the armpit especially in those whose muscles are thin because it comes out quickly and goes back in quickly.
And as for those whose muscles are many, it is the opposite, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty. And perhaps some people will suffer a blow or fall and the scapula will become swollen and hot, so they may think that it has broken and should be checked to ensure its safety.
When, therefore, you treat it, the mandible will advance. The jawbone is known if it goes down towards the armpit. If you compare the broken acromion with a healthy one, you will find between them an apparent discrepancy and in the acromion you will feel a lump of the head of the bone, while under the armpit on touching there is the head of the bone as if it were an egg.
And the skilled physician cannot raise his hand to his ear nor move it in all its movements, and similarly if it is fractured towards the chest or upwards you will find this apparent on touching, not hidden.
And this mandible may be easily reduced if it is soft or the physician is a youth, and its reduction is that you make a servant raise his hand up then place your fingertips underneath his armpit and lift the joint with force upwards to its place and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then you lower it down and it returns quickly; if it does not return as we mentioned, since the mandible has been in that condition for many days, then the physician should bathe himself in hot water and use natron which softens and becomes like it being cooked just like the throat lozenge, the fig and the crown in the water and use it.
Then the physician lies on his back and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and hardness then the doctor places his elbow on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his own hand and extends it downwards.
He pushes the ball with his leg and it returns to its place; and if you wish, reduce it in this manner which is that you prepare a leg longer than the patient's and grasp him from the side and insert your acromion under the patient's armpit and lift up his armpit upwards until the patient is hanging in the air. Another attendant pulls the patient's hand down to his belly. If the patient is slight, another thing should be hung on him so that it will distract him, for he may return the jaw (forceps) from its place, and also he may turn back to another face.
And it is to place in the ground a long beam, its head being round like the stone of a sling, not thick nor thin, then under the patient's arm. The patient stands on the length of the beam.
Then he extends his hand down from the other side and extends his body also from that side with force. Then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult for him to return by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a beam of length approximately two arms long. And its width is as much as four fingers, and it thickens as much as two fingers. Its head being round so that it can enter the depth of the armpit in this way:.
Then a linen cloth is tied on the round head so that it does not harm the beam. Then he places it under the armpit, and extends his hand and arm all together down onto the beam, and ties the beam to the elbow and forearm and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a support, with the support raised high and the hand extended downwards. The rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters into its place, so if it goes in easily by any means possible, then a ball of moderate size made of wool is to be put under the armpit.
Then the prepared poultice, mixed with frankincense and egg white, is carried on the shoulder in a circular motion from above. Then the ball is pulled tightly under the armpit and tied with a bandage over the poultice. His hand is then tied to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be a little less until the member strengthens. Then, after seven or five days, he should be exercised by movement; if it stabilizes and does not ache, then he is healed.
If the joint is frequently dislocated due to dampness or another affliction, it is advisable to apply a poultice of the three saffron-infused pastes as mentioned in the chapter on poultices. If all this has been done and the ligament has been loosened after seven days but the joint does not stabilize.
The poultice and bandage were reapplied several times, but it did not stabilize and fell and became flaccid and was unable to be lifted up. So know that its nerve, which is at the head of the humerus, has been cut off or stretched out or relaxed. So know then that the joint will never stabilize in its place ever.
And as for the jaw that is towards the chest and breast, and behind its separation it should be pushed and stretched with the hands until it returns. And all of the bandages and treatments are used in it until it heals. If a lump appears in the member after healing and it moves slowly.
The patient should repeatedly apply hot water to it until that lump softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the treatment of the shoulder joint.
The joint of the shoulder dislocates easily and also returns easily, and it dislocates in all directions especially to the front or behind, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is visible and palpable in any shape in which it dislocates.
If you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound one, it will be clear to you by its appearance and looseness of the joint that it cannot be bent or lifted with the humerus, and you should hurry and reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling appears.
If a swelling appears on him that is hot and difficult to reduce, he will not be completely cured, especially if it is behind the jawbone, for it becomes more severe in all types of jaw swellings and more painful. Many people have died with this condition.
And if it is something that can be reduced, have a servant extend his hand with both hands and arm relaxed, the physician's hand from above the elbow and beneath it, and he pushes the joint with the tips of his fingers together or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
And as for the jawbone being forward, it can be reduced by bending the hand once so that its base knocks against the angled part alongside it. If the jawbone does not yield to returning, use a strong forceful push, and have servants extend their arm.
And the physician should also have servants hold him, so that he does not move when being pushed. Then his arm is turned in all directions after a cloth or a wide turban has been wrapped around it. And if the physician begins with both hands, the joint is rubbed with grease, to make the movement of the joint easier.
Then the joint is pushed firmly until it returns, and after its return a poultice is applied to it that contains drying and thickening with egg white, and it is tightly tied. The arm is hung from the patient's neck and left for some days.
Then, if the joint has remained in its place, the bandage is removed from it and left. And if you see that the joint has not been tightened enough, the poultice and bandage are reapplied and left for some days until it tightens up. Then it is removed, and if a swelling occurs after the joint's return and removal of the bandage,
And if there is difficulty in movement, moistening and gentle rubbing and anointing are applied in the bath until it becomes supple. Or a fat sheep's tail is placed on the joint and tied to it and left for a day and night. Then it is removed and he enters the bath, and when sweat appears, rub the joint with a moderate amount of rubbing.
Then repeat the application three times, after entering the bath until it softens. If you wish to make the fat of a cow liquid and subtle with the fat, do so several times; it will soften and return to its original state.
The twenty-eighth chapter.
For the treatment of a dislocated shoulder joint.
The shoulder joint of the hand is often dislocated and returning it to its place becomes easy compared to other joints, except that it should be hastened to return it to its place immediately when it becomes dislocated before a redness or inflammation occurs in the area.
Return the dislocated shoulder by placing the patient's shoulder on a board, and having an assistant extend their hand and the physician put his own hand on the point of the joint and push it until it returns, unless the dislocation has gone into the palm.
Let the patient place their hand's palm on the board during the push and pull, and if the dislocation is prominent to the surface of the hand, let his inner hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same point of the joint. Then let it return immediately; otherwise, tie him with a soothing compress for the swelling.
And leave him, he should not repeat it; for he cannot endure or you can return it to its place after days have passed except that the joint will remain dislocated and nothing will harm the patient except if the hand becomes stiff and is unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve has been cut or injured, there is no remedy for it except to bind him with catgut; perhaps it will be beneficial and perhaps it will not help at all. If the shoulder joint is returned, then apply the compress which we have described, tie it, and leave him for five days.
Then he will heal and the hand will be trained. If its movement becomes difficult and something from gangrene appears in it, then wash it with warm water and vinegar several times until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the treatment of finger fractures.
The fingers may fracture in every direction; when a finger fractures towards the front of the palm or to its back, extend it and push the break with your thumb until it returns; then tie the base of the finger and suspend it towards the side from which it fractured.
Then leave it for two days, then let it go and extend it until it becomes straight; if it was at night, tie it as described above; it will still be in line by day and exercise it with movement, tying it at night. Do that for some days until it is strong.
And do the same with it if it has fractured towards the back of the hand, tie it to about the same side and do with it what you did first until it heals; and do likewise with it whenever it has fractured in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of a fracture of the scapula vertebra.
When there is a fracture of one of the vertebrae in the scapula or neck, whether it is a complete fracture or many vertebrae have come loose from their places, then there is no treatment for it because death approaches quickly.
And a sign of it is that the patient's stool comes out without will, and he cannot hold onto it; often his limbs or arms, or one of them, become weak. And if one vertebra becomes dislocated from its place then many times they also come loose.
And their dislocation is to four sides, the one that moves towards behind is called a hump; and as for its treatment, look, if the hump has occurred from childhood then there is no treatment for it nor any cure whatsoever. And if it happened due to a fall or blow and the like.
We have mentioned it by the name of treatment in long words which most of its benefit has returned and we shortened it from that, what is enough for a little about much they came with from simplification to the meaning and explanation for it and I have shaped the instrument contrary to what they mentioned and explained.
I say that the hernia which occurs in front of the chest, there is no cure for it and no remedy from it, also the one that occurs to both sides. And I treat only the one that occurs on the back, specifically by having him stretch out his face.
On a horizontal beam close to a wall, place a soft mattress under him so that his chest is not harmed. Then put a plank standing upright into a hole in the ground near his head and another plank near his feet on the other side of the beam.
A servant holds the plank and let it be unsecured in the hole. A servant also holds the other plank on this shape. Then wrap a soft bandage around his chest and under his arms, and extend its end to the plank near his head and tie him to it.
Then pull another bandage over his thigh and his knees, and around the back of them. Then gather all the bands together and tie them to the other plank near his feet. Then each servant will extend towards him the plank with the bandages, without moving the planks from their fixed places.
Unless they are not fixed as we said, the doctor will place his hands with force on the pin until it is returned or he will put a board on it and then lean on the board with his feet until it is returned. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board that is three arms long.
And dig in the wall near the patient a place for the end of the board. Then put the middle part of the board on the hernia and the doctor will put his feet on the other end and pull strongly until the pin is compressed.
It returns to its place, and if you wish to make it with the tourniquet which is tied by hand, that is by inserting into the ground at the head of the ulcer in the last incision two wooden sticks each one arm's length, and at its feet also two wooden sticks.
And let there be a space of one span between each wooden stick, and it has been made in every stick a hole through which the tourniquet passes, and all the wood is fastened to the ground firmly so that not even a hair's breadth moves. And enter a turned over staff, which is the tourniquet wrapped around it, into both holes of the wooden sticks.
And at its end there is a hole which has been fastened with a staff of one span in length wrapped around it, and similarly in the other two wooden sticks. Then draw the bandage that you tied at the chest of the ulcer into the tourniquet that is near its head, and the ligatures that you tied at its legs into the tourniquet that is near its feet.
Then a servant stands near each tourniquet and tightens it with the vice which turns around it, and the physician sets the swelling as we have mentioned. And this is the image of the tourniquet, the wooden splints and the ulcer:.
Then after the splints have been returned and the place has been evened, it is necessary to carry the dried poultice with white egg and vinegar. Then put a splint from a board on top of the poultice which will be three fingers in width or so, and its length will take the place of the swelling.
And on top of some correct splints then tie with the bandage that is needed. And let the ulcer eat the gentle food until it heals, if there remains some nodules in the place at the end of the healing, it is necessary to use the medicines that are dissolved and softened along with the wooden splint that we described for a long time.
And it is used for that a lead plate, and there may be an abscess at the end of the vertebrae in the back which they think is a perforation and it will be a redundant bone that has become inflamed so you should not expose it to this treatment lest death occur.
The Thirty-first Chapter.
For the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the knee are only dislocated in them (i.e. the bone can be dislocated) and not dislocated in them as is the case with other joints, where they slip out or fall apart, and the joint of the hip dislocates on four sides.
And that is because it dislocates inward and outward and forward and backward, and more often does it dislocate inward, and less often does it dislocate forward or backward, and a sign of its dislocation inward is that if you compare the healthy leg with the diseased one, the diseased one will be longer.
And the knee will be bent more than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg when sitting down, and the place which is behind the crease and is warm and hot, will be clear of what was there before the head of the femur had moved to that place.
And a sign of its dislocation outward is that the symptoms are opposite to these symptoms, and a sign of its dislocation forward is that he will extend his leg completely except that he cannot bend it without pain, and if he tries to walk he will not be able to do so in front.
And it will be in a state of retention, and he will be unable to control his urine, and he will tremble at the crease, and when walking his heel will strike the ground, and a sign of its dislocation backward is that he will not be able to extend the knee nor bend it before bending the crease, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
The thighs should be relaxed and the prominence of the groin at the site where the inguinal hernia is present. As for the treatment of this rupture, look: if the rupture has long since worn down its owner, and he did not try to reduce it, and remained in its state, then there is no cure for it whatsoever.
There is no need to treat it. As for the rupture which has a recent onset, and whatever type it was from among the four forms of rupture, move the joint that is connected with it towards the inside and outside, and turn it left and right. Perhaps.
He should return to his normal state without the need for further treatment. If he does not, it is necessary that a strong servant holds him fast with both arms, and another servant supports him from above.
He should enter his hands under his armpits, then he should be held fast with a soft strap at the base of the groin. And another servant holds the end of the strap and another third servant is holding him either from in front of the thighs towards the knee or from behind towards the back.
And they all hold him at the same time until the rupture is lifted up by his body from the ground and remains hanging. If this type of support is used, it is a common treatment for all four types. If the rupture returns as we said, or else there must be a specific treatment for each type mentioned.
As for the specific treatment, if the rupture is inside, the patient should lie on his healthy side then a strap is wrapped around the base of the groin between the prominence of the groin and the place underneath it.
Then the strap is stretched upwards from the groin area to the top of the body towards the knee. And another strong servant takes hold with both arms and holds the swollen part from the inside of the groin and stretches it outwards very tightly until it returns to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment by which this member can be returned, if it was difficult for you and did not respond to entering this type of treatment at all, then the patient's two legs should be bound with a strong yet thin bandage on the ankles and knees.
And it will be with each of its two companions the length of four fingers, and the healthy leg will be stretched more than the other by two fingers, then the patient will be hung from the head of a beam which is in the house and it will be above the ground by the length of two arms.
Then order a strong youth to embrace the patient's thigh and hang him from another youth, then push the other youth with force who is embracing the thigh, in this way the joint will return to its place quickly, and as for his own reduction, if it was outside of his socket.
It is necessary that the patient lies on the wooden boards as we described for the one with a dislocation, and the bandage should be tightened tightly on his healthy leg, and on his chest, then two planks are placed next to his feet and another next to his head, then an additional plank is placed in the middle of the wooden boards very securely.
It may be wrapped with a wet cloth to prevent the patient from being harmed, so that the plank is between his thighs, lest he slides down when he bends, then each servant extends towards him and the doctor adjusts the hip with his hands, if it has responded to returning.
If not, then place the plank on him and secure it as we mentioned for dislocation, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side, and if the dislocation is in front, then extend the patient's leg once while he is in this position on the wooden boards.
The doctor places the back of his right hand on the upper femur, then presses it with his other hand, and while doing so he extends the pressure downward towards the knee, and if the dislocation is at the back, it should not be extended downward.
He should be elevated above the ground, but rather it is suitable that he be placed on a solid object. Also, his foot should be relaxed outside in accordance with what we have mentioned of his being twisted on the board and he on his face with the bandages tightened.
As we have said before, it is suitable that the pad be used on the board also on the place where the joint has come out to, if the return of the hip joint becomes complete as it should and its sign is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them being steady and the patient grasping his leg and extending it without any difficulty, know that the joint has returned as it should. Then tie the thighs together and hold the pad and bind with a bandage tightly so that the hip does not move to one side or the other.
The patient needs rest. Three days or four is required for it, then the bandage and pad are released and the leg is measured with the other. If you see them equal in measurement, know that the joint has been stabilized, so release the patient to walk, if however you see something of relaxation in him.
Then return and reapply the pad, bind it as you bound it first, and leave him for three days. Then release it and he will walk on it with a little slowness for some days until his leg becomes strong, if God willing.
The second and third chapters.
In the treatment of a dislocation of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: it dislocates inward, outward and downward, meaning backward. And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg towards his hip.
If it does not adhere to the hip, know that the knee is dislocated. Then correct all faces of its dislocation. Have the patient sit on his back with his legs straight if he can, and a bearer sits behind him to hold his middle and bend him slightly backward, then you sit on his thighs.
Hold your back against his chest and place his leg between yours, then grasp the calf of his leg with both hands and intertwine the fingers of each hand among those of the other, firmly compressing the knee with both hands. Have another servant stretch out his foot until the kneecap returns to its natural position.
And a sign that it has returned is that the thigh will become loose and relaxed, not forcibly. Then grasp them firmly and tie them together with bandages for three or four days then release them. And he should only perform a little walking until they become strong again.
If you find it difficult to return her as we have described, then use the strong traction with bandages that I mentioned in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the Treatment of an Ankle Sprain.
The ankle may slip away slightly or become completely dislocated, either inwardly or outwardly. A sign of its dislocation is that the ankle will be swollen and prominent in the direction it has dislocated to.
And as for the treatment of its slipping away, it is easy to return it and that is by gently straightening it with both hands until it returns. And as for the treatment of its being completely dislocated, it is necessary for the physician to sit the patient down back-first and have a strong servant hold him from behind his back in the middle.
Then take hold of his right hand with your right hand from the top of it, and with your left hand from the bottom of his foot in the position of the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand gently towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we described, then in the third time push the front of the foot towards the leg while you are pulling with the heel, if it returns in one or two attempts and you see the foot straightened, otherwise repeat the work on it because it will return.
If the patient refuses to return as we have described, then place the patient on his back on the ground and insert a splint firmly into the ground so that it falls between his thighs. A mat should be placed under him so that he is not hurt by the splint. Then the servant will tie his foot.
Then another servant will extend the man by his hands or with a rope tied around the man's neck. Then each servant will extend in opposition to the other one and the splint will be standing between the patient's thighs holding him so that he does not pull down when being extended, then the physician will straighten the jaw with his hands.
And another servant will hold the healthy leg down. If the jaw returns quickly and it is clear to you that it has returned correctly, then take the splint and traction and bind it with tight straps and secure the foot by a strap downwards. It should be avoided from the nerve which is behind the heel.
So that the strap may not be too tight and harm him, then leave him for two or three days. If the strap has relaxed then tighten it again and release it in the third or fourth day. The patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If the patient attempts to walk before this period then I fear that the jaw may break and be ruined, and he will not accept treatment thereafter. If a hot inflammation appears, it is necessary to use what we have described for its relief in another place of treatment and dressing until it subsides.
The 34th Chapter.
Treatment of the jaw fracture of the man's hand.
It is necessary to straighten what has been bent by a short stretch without force, which is not difficult but easy. If the jaw fracture occurs in some parts of the heel then it is necessary that the patient places his foot on a level place on the ground.
If he stands on a board, as if he were walking, then you come and place your foot on the part that has been dislocated from those joints. Then press it with your foot with great force until it goes back in place and does not appear again at that location.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, taking all of it so that there are two heads to it. Then pull it tight with great force and security for three days. Then cover it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you can prevent it from returning, if God wills.
The Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
In the types of fracture of the jaw, whether with a wound or a break, or both together.
If something like this has happened and his treatment and cure are neglected, it often results in death.
Therefore, it is not fitting for anyone to attempt a treatment like this unless he is skilled in the craft, long experienced, gentle, calm and deliberate, without haste or rashness, and first uses medicines that quiet down the inflamed ulcers alone.
Praise to God for what I have hoped for him, safety from the injury with a mild sickness, and I saw in him some hope so return it from your time in the beginning before the inflamed ulcer occurs.
If the limb returns to what you wanted, then use the treatment that quiets the inflamed ulcers and treat the wound with ointments that are dryed, if the jaw is broken and there are splinters of bone that have been exposed, remove them. And agree with what we mentioned in simple illnesses, as stated in their places.
And strive with effort and cleanse yourself from entering the path of error, as you advanced and my advice to you, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, if God wills.
